[14/01/24, 9:02:17â€¯PM] ~â€¯Aronyak: I have not worked on the project yet but just doing some basic eda reveals the skewness in the data. Also for such a classification model, I feel like trying ensemble gradient boosted trees would yield satisfactory results as I have learnt from my exp. The low accuracy for most submissions is understandable because I think to extract the information from the review text, it's better to use a Naive Bayes or gaussian naive bayesian approach to predict text sentiment. Also, imo, the ID columns won't yield any significant feature importance so better to drop.
One might also try a neural net based approach for this.

[15/01/24, 12:07:59â€¯PM] ~â€¯Ganesh P: The problem you are trying to solve is class imbalance I believe.
Skew has a different meaning na.. Skew is a term associated with continuous data. That's where these transformations help.
If you find a feature is heavily skewed, you will transform it.

Here, the problem is class imbalance in labels.
[15/01/24, 12:08:30â€¯PM] ~â€¯Ganesh P: Exactly what I am trying to say

[15/01/24, 12:12:15â€¯PM] ~â€¯Lovin Tanwar: Yes, I guess 80-90 percent of efforts will go down in pre-processing
[15/01/24, 12:12:36â€¯PM] ~â€¯Lovin Tanwar: Specially handling class imbalance issue

[15/01/24, 12:15:53â€¯PM] ~â€¯Ganesh P: Then try to summarize

How many rows?
How many features?
How many classes in label? What is their distribution? 
Which column has unique values?
What is the distribution of each continuous feature? 
How many categories and how are they distributed for each cat var?
Read description of each variable and try to understand qualitatively how it will affect the label i.e., rating?
Think like a domain expert and make a list of variables which will be important (before statistical approaches).
Then use statistical approaches, and see it they match your expert views. If there's a difference, try to understand why there's a difference.
Then see how each var is related to label.
Understand what label 0 means and how important it is. If in doubt, post in discourse.
And so on

[28/01/24, 8:01:51â€¯PM] ~â€¯Anmaya Panda: df = pd.read_csv("/kaggle/input/recipe-for-rating-predict-food-ratings-using-ml/train.csv")
X = df.drop("Rating", axis=1)
y = df['Rating']

from sklearn.dummy import DummyClassifier
model = DummyClassifier().fit(X,y)

X_test = pd.read_csv("/kaggle/input/recipe-for-rating-predict-food-ratings-using-ml/test.csv")
y_pred=model.predict(X_test)

submission = pd.DataFrame({"ID": range(1,4547), 
                           "total_amount": y_pred}) 

submission.to_csv('submission.csv',index=False) 
just run this code in your notebook....

[09/02/24, 12:01:35â€¯PM] ~â€¯Yashi: df = pd.read_csv("/kaggle/input/recipe-for-rating-predict-food-ratings-using-ml/train.csv")
X = df.drop("Rating", axis=1)
y = df['Rating']

from sklearn.dummy import DummyClassifier
model = DummyClassifier().fit(X,y)

X_test = pd.read_csv("/kaggle/input/recipe-for-rating-predict-food-ratings-using-ml/test.csv")
y_pred=model.predict(X_test)

submission = pd.DataFrame({"ID": range(1,4547), 
                           "total_amount": y_pred}) 

submission.to_csv('submission.csv',index=False)

[29/02/24, 9:38:54â€¯PM] ~â€¯Sonu Singh: Hi, my submission just crossed the cutoff and here is something that you can learn from my silly mistake and there is a bonus tip at the end.

Here is what I was doing:
1. Split data into train and test.
2. Use the GridSearchCV over the train-data to tune the parameters and train the estimator.
3. Then evaluate the performance on the test-data.
4.  Submit the predictions done with the same estimator. Score: 75.978%

But that silly mistake is step 4. Here is the correct approach:
4. After hyperparameter tuning and your best model ready, apply the preprocessing pipeline to the entire data from the train.csv file and then freshly train you model with best estimator on this transformed entire-data. Then use this model to predict and submit the score. Score: 77.276%

As you can see by correcting this mistake there was a huge jump in my score so, if you are doing the same mistake like me then, just follow my advice and try to train the model on entire dataset after finding the best parameters.

Tip: Hyperparameter tuning is a real pain, it takes a lot of time to finish, so you can duplicate your Kaggle notebook and run multiple notebooks at same time. Let's say you want to find the best value of the parameter 'l1_ratio' then you can have the value  0.2 and 04 in parameter grid of one notebook and 0.6 and 0.8 in another notebook and both running simultaneously thus taking less time to find the best parameters. I don't know the upper limit on the number of notebooks we can run parallelly but I have tested this with 2 notebooks and it works fine. You can also try that on colab and with different accounts. Therefore you can have 10 to 20 different  notebooks running simultaneously finding best parameters for your model.

I hope it helps. ğŸ˜Š
